`Oh! that some kind spirit would whisper those words in his (Weston) ear,' I inwardly exclaimed. I was far too indignant to hazard a reply to her (Weston) observation aloud; and nothing more was said about Mr. Weston that day, by me or in my hearing. But next morning, soon after breakfast, Miss Murray came into the schoolroom, where her (Murray) sister was employed at her (Murray) studies, or rather her (Murray) lessons, for studies they were not, and said, `Matilda, I want you to take a walk with me about eleven o’clock.'

`Oh, I can't, Rosalie! I have to give orders about my new bridle and saddle-cloth, and speak to the rat-catcher about his (Weston) dogs: Miss Grey must go with you.'

`No, I want you,' said Rosalie; and calling her (Rosalie) sister to the window, she (Rosalie) whispered an explanation in her (Rosalie) ear; upon which the latter consented to go.

I remembered that eleven was the hour at which Mr. Weston proposed to come to the porter's lodge; and remembering that, I beheld the whole contrivance. Accordingly, at dinner, I was entertained with a long account of how Mr. Weston had overtaken them as they were walking along the road; and how they had had a long walk and talk with him (Weston), and really found him (Weston) quite an agreeable companion; and how he (Weston) must have been, and evidently was, delighted with them and their amazing condescension, &c. &c.

CHAPTER XVII--CONFESSIONS

As I am in the way of confessions I may as well acknowledge that, about this time, I paid more attention to dress than ever I had done before. This is not saying much--for hitherto I had been a little neglectful in that particular; but now, also, it was no uncommon thing to spend as much as two minutes in the contemplation of my own image in the glass; though I never could derive any consolation from such a study. I could discover no beauty in those marked features, that pale hollow cheek, and ordinary dark brown hair; there might be intellect in the forehead, there might be expression in the dark grey eyes, but what of that?--a low Grecian brow, and large black eyes devoid of sentiment would be esteemed far preferable. It is foolish to wish for beauty. Sensible people never either desire it for themselves or care about it in others. If the mind be but well cultivated, and the heart well disposed, no one ever cares for the exterior. So said the teachers of our childhood; and so say we to the children of the present day. All very judicious and proper, no doubt; but are such assertions supported by actual experience?

We are naturally disposed to love what gives us pleasure, and what more pleasing than a beautiful face--when we know no harm of the possessor at least? A little girl loves her (girl) bird--Why? Because it lives and feels; because it is helpless and harmless? A toad, likewise, lives and feels, and is equally helpless and harmless; but though she (girl) would not hurt a toad, she (girl) cannot love it like the bird, with its graceful form, soft feathers, and bright, speaking eyes. If a woman is fair and amiable, she (woman) is praised for both qualities, but especially the former, by the bulk of mankind: if, on the other hand, she (woman) is disagreeable in person and character, her (woman) plainness is commonly inveighed against as her (woman) greatest crime, because, to common observers, it gives the greatest offence; while, if she (woman) is plain and good, provided she (woman) is a person of retired manners and secluded life, no one ever knows of her (woman) goodness, except her (woman) immediate connections. Others, on the contrary, are disposed to form unfavourable opinions of her (woman) mind, and disposition, if it be but to excuse themselves for their instinctive dislike of one so unfavoured by nature; and _visa versâ_ with her (woman) whose angel form conceals a vicious heart, or sheds a false, deceitful charm over defects and foibles that would not be tolerated in another. They that have beauty, let them be thankful for it, and make a good use of it, like any other talent; they that have it not, let them console themselves, and do the best they can without it: certainly, though liable to be over-estimated, it is a gift of God, and not to be despised. Many will feel this who have felt that they could love, and whose hearts tell them that they are worthy to be loved again; while yet they are debarred, by the lack of this or some such seeming trifle, from giving and receiving that happiness they seem almost made to feel and to impart. As well might the humble glowworm despise that power of giving light without which the roving fly might pass her (they) and repass her (they) a thousand times, and never rest beside her (they): she (they) might hear her (they) winged darling buzzing over and around her (they); he (they) vainly seeking her (they), she (they) longing to be found, but with no power to make her (they) presence known, no voice to call him (they), no wings to follow his (they) flight;--the fly must seek another mate, the worm must live and die alone.

Such were some of my reflections about this period. I might go on prosing more and more, I might dive much deeper, and disclose other thoughts, propose questions the reader might be puzzled to answer, and deduce arguments that might startle his (reader) prejudices, or, perhaps, provoke his (reader) ridicule, because he (reader) could not comprehend them; but I forbear.

Now, therefore, let us return to Miss Murray. She (Murray) accompanied her (Murray) mamma to the ball on Tuesday; of course splendidly attired, and delighted with her (Murray) prospects and her (Murray) charms. As Ashby Park was nearly ten miles distant from Horton Lodge, they had to set out pretty early, and I intended to have spent the evening with Nancy Brown, whom I had not seen for a long time; but my kind pupil took care I should spend it neither there nor anywhere else beyond the limits of the schoolroom, by giving me a piece of music to copy, which kept me closely occupied till bed-time. About eleven next morning, as soon as she (Brown) had left her (Brown) room, she (Brown) came to tell me her (Brown) news. Sir Thomas had indeed proposed to her (Brown) at the ball; an event which reflected great credit on her (Brown) mamma's sagacity, if not upon her (Brown) skill in contrivance. I rather incline to the belief that she (Brown) had first laid her (Brown) plans, and then predicted their success. The offer had been accepted, of course, and the bridegroom elect was coming that day to settle matters with Mr. Murray.

Rosalie was pleased with the thoughts of becoming mistress of Ashby Park; she (Rosalie) was elated with the prospect of the bridal ceremony and its attendant splendour and éclat, the honeymoon spent abroad, and the subsequent gaieties she (Rosalie) expected to enjoy in London and elsewhere; she (Rosalie) appeared pretty well pleased too, for the time being, with Sir Thomas himself, because she (Rosalie) had so lately seen him (Thomas), danced with him (Thomas), and been flattered by him (Thomas); but, after all, she (Rosalie) seemed to shrink from the idea of being so soon united: she (Rosalie) wished the ceremony to be delayed some months, at least; and I wished it too. It seemed a horrible thing to hurry on the inauspicious match, and not to give the poor creature time to think and reason on the irrevocable step she (Rosalie) was about to take. I made no pretension to `a mother's watchful, anxious care,' but I was amazed and horrified at Mrs. Murray's heartlessness, or want of thought for the real good of her (Murray) child; and by my unheeded warnings and exhortations, I vainly strove to remedy the evil. Miss Murray only laughed at what I said; and I soon found that her (Murray) reluctance to an immediate union arose chiefly from a desire to do what execution she (Murray) could among the young gentlemen of her (Murray) acquaintance, before she (Murray) was incapacitated from further mischief of the kind. It was for this cause that, before confiding to me the secret of her (Murray) engagement, she (Murray) had extracted a promise that I would not mention a word on the subject to any one. And when I saw this, and when I beheld her (Murray) plunge more recklessly than ever into the depths of heartless coquetry, I had no more pity for her (Murray). `Come what will,' I thought, `she (Murray) deserves it. Sir Thomas cannot be too bad for her (Murray); and the sooner she (Murray) is incapacitated from deceiving and injuring others the better.'

The wedding was fixed for the first of June. Between that and the critical ball was little more than six weeks; but, with Rosalie's accomplished skill and resolute exertion, much might be done, even within that period; especially as Sir Thomas spent most of the interim in London; whither he (Thomas) went up, it was said, to settle affairs with his (Thomas) lawyer, and make other preparations for the approaching nuptials. He (Thomas) endeavoured to supply the want of his (Thomas) presence by a pretty constant fire of billets-doux; but these did not attract the neighbours' attention, and open their eyes, as personal visits would have done; and old Lady Ashby's haughty, sour spirit of reserve withheld her (Ashby) from spreading the news, while her (Ashby) indifferent health prevented her (Ashby) coming to visit her (Ashby) future daughter-in-law; so that, altogether, this affair was kept far closer than such things usually are.

Rosalie would sometimes show her (Rosalie) lover's epistles to me, to convince me what a kind, devoted husband he (husband) would make. She (Rosalie) showed me the letters of another individual, too, the unfortunate Mr. Green, who had not the courage, or, as she (Rosalie) expressed it, the `spunk,' to plead his (Green) cause in person, but whom one denial would not satisfy: he (Green) must write again and again. He (Green) would not have done so if he (Green) could have seen the grimaces his (Green) fair idol made over his (Green) moving appeals to her (Rosalie) feelings, and heard her (Rosalie) scornful laughter, and the opprobrious epithets she (Rosalie) heaped upon him (Green) for his (Green) perseverance.

`Why don't you tell him (Green), at once, that you are engaged?' I asked.

`Oh, I don't want him (Green) to know that,' replied she (Rosalie). `If he (Green) knew it, his (Green) sisters and everybody would know it, and then there would be an end of my--ahem! And, besides, if I told him (Green) that, he (Green) would think my engagement was the only obstacle, and that I would have him (Green) if I were free; which I could not bear that any man should think, and he (man), of all others, at least. Besides, I don't care for his (man) letters,' she (Rosalie) added, contemptuously; `he (man) may write as often as he (man) pleases, and look as great a calf as he (man) likes when I meet him (man); it only amuses me.'

Meantime, young Meltham was pretty frequent in his (Meltham) visits to the house or transits past it; and, judging by Matilda's execrations and reproaches, her (Matilda) sister paid more attention to him (Meltham) than civility required; in other words, she (Matilda) carried on as animated a flirtation as the presence of her (Matilda) parents would admit. She (Matilda) made some attempts to bring Mr. Hatfield once more to her (Matilda) feet; but finding them unsuccessful, she (Matilda) repaid his (Hatfield) haughty indifference with still loftier scorn, and spoke of him (Hatfield) with as much disdain and detestation as she (Matilda) had formerly done of his (Hatfield) curate. But, amid all this, she (Matilda) never for a moment lost sight of Mr. Weston. She (Matilda) embraced every opportunity of meeting him (Weston), tried every art to fascinate him (Weston), and pursued him (Weston) with as much perseverance as if she (Matilda) really loved him (Weston) and no other, and the happiness of her (Matilda) life depended upon eliciting a return of affection. Such conduct was completely beyond my comprehension. Had I seen it depicted in a novel, I should have thought it unnatural; had I heard it described by others, I should have deemed it a mistake or an exaggeration; but when I saw it with my own eyes, and suffered from it too, I could only conclude that excessive vanity, like drunkenness, hardens the heart, enslaves the faculties, and perverts the feelings; and that dogs are not the only creatures which, when gorged to the throat, will yet gloat over what they cannot devour, and grudge the smallest morsel to a starving brother.

She (it) now became extremely beneficent to the poor cottagers. Her (it) acquaintance among them was more widely extended, her (it) visits to their humble dwellings were more frequent and excursive than they had ever been before. Hereby, she (it) earned among them the reputation of a condescending and very charitable young lady; and their encomiums were sure to be repeated to Mr. Weston: whom also she (lady) had thus a daily chance of meeting in one or other of their abodes, or in her (lady) transits to and fro; and often, likewise, she (lady) could gather, through their gossip, to what places he (Weston) was likely to go at such and such a time, whether to baptize a child, or to visit the aged, the sick, the sad, or the dying; and most skilfully she (lady) laid her (lady) plans accordingly. In these excursions she (lady) would sometimes go with her (lady) sister--whom, by some means, she (lady) had persuaded or bribed to enter into her (lady) schemes--sometimes alone, never, now, with me; so that I was debarred the pleasure of seeing Mr. Weston, or hearing his (Weston) voice even in conversation with another: which would certainly have been a very great pleasure, however hurtful or however fraught with pain. I could not even see him (Weston) at church: for Miss Murray, under some trivial pretext, chose to take possession of that corner in the family pew which had been mine ever since I came; and, unless I had the presumption to station myself between Mr. and Mrs. Murray, I must sit with my back to the pulpit, which I accordingly did.

Now, also, I never walked home with my pupils: they said their mamma thought it did not look well to see three people out of the family walking, and only two going in the carriage; and, as they greatly preferred walking in fine weather, I should be honoured by going with the seniors. `And besides,' said they, `you can't walk as fast as we do; you know you're always lagging behind.' I knew these were false excuses, but I made no objections, and never contradicted such assertions, well knowing the motives which dictated them. And in the afternoons, during those six memorable weeks, I never went to church at all. If I had a cold, or any slight indisposition, they took advantage of that to make me stay at home; and often they would tell me they were not going again that day, themselves, and then pretend to change their minds, and set off without telling me: so managing their departure that I never discovered the change of purpose till too late. Upon their return home, on one of these occasions, they entertained me with an animated account of a conversation they had had with Mr. Weston as they came along. `And he (Weston) asked if you were ill, Miss Grey,' said Matilda; `but we told him (Weston) you were quite well, only you didn't want to come to church--so he (Weston)'ll think you're turned wicked.'

All chance meetings on week-days were likewise carefully prevented; for, lest I should go to see poor Nancy Brown or any other person, Miss Murray took good care to provide sufficient employment for all my leisure hours. There was always some drawing to finish, some music to copy, or some work to do, sufficient to incapacitate me from indulging in anything beyond a short walk about the grounds, however she (Murray) or her (Murray) sister might be occupied.

One morning, having sought and waylaid Mr. Weston, they returned in high glee to give me an account of their interview. `And he (Weston) asked after you again,' said Matilda, in spite of her (Matilda) sister's silent but imperative intimation that she (Matilda) should hold her (Matilda) tongue. `He (Weston) wondered why you were never with us, and thought you must have delicate health, as you came out so seldom.'

`He (Weston) didn't Matilda--what nonsense you're talking!'

`Oh, Rosalie, what a lie! He (Weston) did, you know; and you said--Don't, Rosalie--hang it!--I won't be pinched so! And, Miss Grey, Rosalie told him (Weston) you were quite well, but you were always so buried in your books that you had no pleasure in anything else.'

`What an idea he (Weston) must have of me!' I thought.

`And,' I asked, `does old Nancy ever inquire about me?'

`Yes; and we tell her (Nancy) you are so fond of reading and drawing that you can do nothing else.'

`That is not the case though; if you had told her (Nancy) I was so busy I could not come to see her (Nancy), it would have been nearer the truth.'

`I don't think it would,' replied Miss Murray, suddenly kindling up; `I'm sure you have plenty of time to yourself now, when you have so little teaching to do.'

It was no use beginning to dispute with such indulged, unreasoning creatures: so I held my peace. I was accustomed, now, to keeping silence when things distasteful to my ear were uttered; and now, too, I was used to wearing a placid smiling countenance when my heart was bitter within me. Only those who have felt the like can imagine my feelings, as I sat with an assumption of smiling indifference, listening to the accounts of those meetings and interviews with Mr. Weston, which they seemed to find such pleasure in describing to me; and hearing things asserted of him (Weston) which, from the character of the man, I knew to be exaggerations and perversions of the truth, if not entirely false--things derogatory to him (man), and flattering to them--especially to Miss Murray--which I burned to contradict, or, at least, to show my doubts about, but dared not; lest, in expressing my disbelief, I should display my interest too. Other things I heard, which I felt or feared were indeed too true: but I must still conceal my anxiety respecting him (man), my indignation against them, beneath a careless aspect; others, again, mere hints of something said or done, which I longed to hear more of, but could not venture to inquire. So passed the weary time. I could not even comfort myself with saying, `She (them) will soon be married; and then there may be hope.'

Soon after her (them) marriage the holidays would come; and when I returned from home, most likely, Mr. Weston would be gone, for I was told that he (Weston) and the Rector could not agree -LRB-the Rector's fault, of course-RRB-, and he (Rector) was about to remove to another place.

No--besides my hope in God, my only consolation was in thinking that, though he (God) know it not, I was more worthy of his (God) love than Rosalie Murray, charming and engaging as she (Murray) was; for I could appreciate his (God) excellence, which she (Murray) could not: I would devote my life to the promotion of his (God) happiness; she (Murray) would destroy his (God) happiness for the momentary gratification of her (Murray) own vanity. `Oh, if he (God) could but know the difference!' I would earnestly exclaim. `But no! I would not have him (God) see my heart: yet, if he (God) could but know her (Murray) hollowness, her (Murray) worthless, heartless frivolity, he (God) would then be safe, and I should be--_almost_ happy, though I might never see him (God) more!'

I fear, by this time, the reader is well nigh disgusted with the folly and weakness I have so freely laid before him (reader). I never disclosed it then, and would not have done so had my own sister or my mother been with me in the house. I was a close and resolute dissembler--in this one case at least. My prayers, my tears, my wishes, fears, and lamentations, were witnessed by myself and heaven alone.

When we are harassed by sorrows or anxieties, or long oppressed by any powerful feelings which we must keep to ourselves, for which we can obtain and seek no sympathy from any living creature, and which yet we cannot, or will not wholly crush, we often naturally seek relief in poetry--and often find it, too--whether in the effusions of others, which seem to harmonize with our existing case, or in our own attempts to give utterance to those thoughts and feelings in strains less musical, perchance, but more appropriate, and therefore more penetrating and sympathetic, and, for the time, more soothing, or more powerful to rouse and to unburden the oppressed and swollen heart. Before this time, at Wellwood House and here, when suffering from home-sick melancholy, I had sought relief twice or thrice at this secret source of consolation; and now I flew to it again, with greater avidity than ever, because I seemed to need it more. I still preserve those relics of past sufferings and experience, like pillars of witness set up in travelling through the vale of life, to mark particular occurrences. The footsteps are obliterated now; the face of the country may be changed; but the pillar is still there, to remind me how all things were when it was reared. Lest the reader should be curious to see any of these effusions, I will favour him (reader) with one short specimen: cold and languid as the lines may seem, it was almost a passion of grief to which they owed their being:--

Oh, they have robbed me of the hope My spirit held so dear; They will not let me hear that voice My soul delights to hear.

They will not let me see that face I so delight to see; And they have taken all thy smiles, And all thy love from me.

Well, let them seize on all they can;-- One treasure still is mine,-- A heart that loves to think on thee, And feels the worth of thine.

Yes, at least, they could not deprive me of that: I could think of him (I) day and night; and I could feel that he (I) was worthy to be thought of. Nobody knew him (I) as I did; nobody could appreciate him (I) as I did; nobody could love him (I) as I--could, if I might: but there was the evil. What business had I to think so much of one that never thought of me? Was it not foolish? was it not wrong? Yet, if I found such deep delight in thinking of him (I), and if I kept those thoughts to myself, and troubled no one else with them, where was the harm of it? I would ask myself. And such reasoning prevented me from making any sufficient effort to shake off my fetters.

But, if those thoughts brought delight, it was a painful, troubled pleasure, too near akin to anguish; and one that did me more injury than I was aware of. It was an indulgence that a person of more wisdom or more experience would doubtless have denied herself. And yet, how dreary to turn my eyes from the contemplation of that bright object and force them to dwell on the dull, grey, desolate prospect around: the joyless, hopeless, solitary path that lay before me. It was wrong to be so joyless, so desponding; I should have made God my friend, and to do His (God) will the pleasure and the business of my life; but faith was weak, and passion was too strong.

In this time of trouble I had two other causes of affliction. The first may seem a trifle, but it cost me many a tear: Snap, my little dumb, rough-visaged, but bright-eyed, warm-hearted companion, the only thing I had to love me, was taken away, and delivered over to the tender mercies of the village rat-catcher, a man notorious for his (man) brutal treatment of his (man) canine slaves. The other was serious enough; my letters from home gave intimation that my father's health was worse. No boding fears were expressed, but I was grown timid and despondent, and could not help fearing that some dreadful calamity awaited us there. I seemed to see the black clouds gathering round my native hills, and to hear the angry muttering of a storm that was about to burst, and desolate our hearth.

CHAPTER XVIII--MIRTH AND MOURNING

The 1st of June arrived at last: and Rosalie Murray was transmuted into Lady Ashby. Most splendidly beautiful she (Ashby) looked in her (Ashby) bridal costume. Upon her (Ashby) return from church, after the ceremony, she (Ashby) came flying into the schoolroom, flushed with excitement, and laughing, half in mirth, and half in reckless desperation, as it seemed to me.

`Now, Miss Grey, I'm Lady Ashby!' she (Ashby) exclaimed. `It's done, my fate is sealed: there's no drawing back now. I'm come to receive your congratulations and bid you good-by; and then I'm off for Paris, Rome, Naples, Switzerland, London--oh, dear! what a deal I shall see and hear before I come back again. But don't forget me: I shan't forget you, though I've been a naughty girl. Come, why don't you congratulate me?'

`I cannot congratulate you,' I replied, `till I know whether this change is really for the better: but I sincerely hope it is; and I wish you true happiness and the best of blessings.'

`Well, good-by, the carriage is waiting, and they're calling me.'

She (girl) gave me a hasty kiss, and was hurrying away; but, suddenly returning, embraced me with more affection than I thought her (girl) capable of evincing, and departed with tears in her (girl) eyes. Poor girl! I really loved her (girl) then; and forgave her (girl) from my heart all the injury she (girl) had done me--and others also: she (girl) had not half known it, I was sure; and I prayed God to pardon her (girl) too.

During the remainder of that day of festal sadness, I was left to my own devices. Being too much unhinged for any steady occupation, I wandered about with a book in my hand for several hours, more thinking than reading, for I had many things to think about. In the evening, I made use of my liberty to go and see my old friend Nancy once again; to apologize for my long absence -LRB-which must have seemed so neglectful and unkind-RRB- by telling her (Nancy) how busy I had been; and to talk, or read, or work for her (Nancy), whichever might be most acceptable, and also, of course, to tell her (Nancy) the news of this important day: and perhaps to obtain a little information from her (Nancy) in return, respecting Mr. Weston's expected departure. But of this she (Nancy) seemed to know nothing, and I hoped, as she (Nancy) did, that it was all a false report. She (Nancy) was very glad to see me; but, happily, her (Nancy) eyes were now so nearly well that she (Nancy) was almost independent of my services. She (Nancy) was deeply interested in the wedding; but while I amused her (Nancy) with the details of the festive day, the splendours of the bridal party and of the bride herself, she (bride) often sighed and shook her (bride) head, and wished good might come of it; she (bride) seemed, like me, to regard it rather as a theme for sorrow than rejoicing. I sat a long time talking to her (bride) about that and other things--but no one came.

Shall I confess that I sometimes looked towards the door with a half-expectant wish to see it open and give entrance to Mr. Weston, as had happened once before? and that, returning through the lanes and fields, I often paused to look round me, and walked more slowly than was at all necessary--for, though a fine evening, it was not a hot one--and, finally, felt a sense of emptiness and disappointment at having reached the house without meeting or even catching a distant glimpse of any one, except a few labourers returning from their work?

Sunday, however, was approaching: I should see him (Weston) then: for now that Miss Murray was gone, I could have my old corner again. I should see him (Weston), and by look, speech, and manner, I might judge whether the circumstance of her (Murray) marriage had very much afflicted him (Weston). Happily I could perceive no shadow of a difference: he (Weston) wore the same aspect as he (Weston) had worn two months ago--voice, look, manner, all alike unchanged: there was the same keen-sighted, unclouded truthfulness in his (Weston) discourse, the same forcible clearness in his (Weston) style, the same earnest simplicity in all he (Weston) said and did, that made itself, not marked by the eye and ear, but felt upon the hearts of his (Weston) audience.

I walked home with Miss Matilda; but _he (Weston) did not join us_. Matilda was now sadly at a loss for amusement, and wofully in want of a companion: her (Matilda) brothers at school, her (Matilda) sister married and gone, she (Matilda) too young to be admitted into society; for which, from Rosalie's example, she (Rosalie) was in some degree beginning to acquire a taste--a taste at least for the company of certain classes of gentlemen; at this dull time of year--no hunting going on, no shooting even--for, though she (Rosalie) might not join in that, it was _something_ to see her (Rosalie) father or the gamekeeper go out with the dogs, and to talk with them on their return, about the different birds they had bagged. Now, also, she (Rosalie) was denied the solace which the companionship of the coachman, grooms, horses, greyhounds, and pointers might have afforded; for her (Rosalie) mother having, notwithstanding the disadvantages of a country life, so satisfactorily disposed of her (Rosalie) elder daughter, the pride of her (Rosalie) heart had begun seriously to turn her (Rosalie) attention to the younger; and, being truly alarmed at the roughness of her (Rosalie) manners, and thinking it high time to work a reform, had been roused at length to exert her (Rosalie) authority, and prohibited entirely the yards, stables, kennels, and coach-house. Of course, she (Rosalie) was not implicitly obeyed; but, indulgent as she (Rosalie) had hitherto been, when once her (Rosalie) spirit was roused, her (Rosalie) temper was not so gentle as she (Rosalie) required that of her (Rosalie) governesses to be, and her (Rosalie) will was not to be thwarted with impunity. After many a scene of contention between mother and daughter, many a violent outbreak which I was ashamed to witness, in which the father's authority was often called in to confirm with oaths and threats the mother's slighted prohibitions--for even _he (father)_ could see that `Tilly, though she (Tilly) would have made a fine lad, was not quite what a young lady ought to be'--Matilda at length found that her (Matilda) easiest plan was to keep clear of the forbidden regions; unless she (Matilda) could now and then steal a visit without her (Matilda) watchful mother's knowledge.

Amid all this, let it not be imagined that I escaped without many a reprimand, and many an implied reproach, that lost none of its sting from not being openly worded; but rather wounded the more deeply, because, from that very reason, it seemed to preclude self-defence. Frequently, I was told to amuse Miss Matilda with other things, and to remind her (Matilda) of her (Matilda) mother's precepts and prohibitions. I did so to the best of my power: but she (Matilda) would not be amused against her (Matilda) will, and could not against her (Matilda) taste; and though I went beyond mere reminding, such gentle remonstrances as I could use were utterly ineffectual.

`_Dear_ Miss Grey! it is the _strangest_ thing. I suppose you can't help it, if it's not in your nature--but I _wonder_ you can't win the confidence of that girl, and make your society at _least_ as agreeable to her (girl) as that of Robert or Joseph!'

`They can talk the best about the things in which she (girl) is most interested,' I replied.

`Well! that is a strange confession, _however_, to come from her (girl) _governess_! Who is to form a young lady's tastes, I wonder, if the governess doesn't do it? I have known governesses who have so completely identified themselves with the reputation of their young ladies for elegance and propriety in mind and manners, that they would blush to speak a word against them; and to hear the slightest blame imputed to their pupils was worse than to be censured in their own persons--and I really think it very natural, for my part.'

`Do you, ma’am?'

`Yes, of course: the young lady's proficiency and elegance is of more consequence to the governess than her (lady) own, as well as to the world. If she (lady) wishes to prosper in her (lady) vocation she (lady) must devote all her (lady) energies to her (lady) business: all her (lady) ideas and all her (lady) ambition will tend to the accomplishment of that one object. When we wish to decide upon the merits of a governess, we naturally look at the young ladies she (lady) professes to have educated, and judge accordingly. The _judicious_ governess knows this: she (lady) knows that, while she (lady) lives in obscurity herself, her (lady) pupils' virtues and defects will be open to every eye; and that, unless she (lady) loses sight of herself in their cultivation, she (lady) need not hope for success. You see, Miss Grey, it is just the same as any other trade or profession: they that wish to prosper must devote themselves body and soul to their calling; and if they begin to yield to indolence or self-indulgence they are speedily distanced by wiser competitors: there is little to choose between a person that ruins her (Grey) pupils by neglect, and one that corrupts them by her (Grey) example. You will excuse my dropping these little hints: you know it is all for your own good. Many ladies would speak to you much more strongly; and many would not trouble themselves to speak at all, but quietly look out for a substitute. That, of course, would be the _easiest_ plan: but I know the advantages of a place like this to a person in your situation; and I have no desire to part with you, as I am sure you would do very well if you will only think of these things and try to exert yourself a _little_ more: then, I am convinced, you would _soon_ acquire that delicate tact which alone is wanting to give you a proper influence over the mind of your pupil.'

I was about to give the lady some idea of the fallacy of her (lady) expectations; but she (lady) sailed away as soon as she (lady) had concluded her (lady) speech. Having said what she (lady) wished, it was no part of her (lady) plan to await my answer: it was my business to hear, and not to speak.

However, as I have said, Matilda at length yielded in some degree to her (Matilda) mother's authority -LRB-pity it had not been exerted before-RRB-; and being thus deprived of almost every source of amusement, there was nothing for it but to take long rides with the groom and long walks with the governess, and to visit the cottages and farmhouses on her (Matilda) father's estate, to kill time in chatting with the old men and women that inhabited them. In one of these walks, it was our chance to meet Mr. Weston. This was what I had long desired; but now, for a moment, I wished either he (Weston) or I were away: I felt my heart throb so violently that I dreaded lest some outward signs of emotion should appear; but I think he (Weston) hardly glanced at me, and I was soon calm enough. After a brief salutation to both, he (Weston) asked Matilda if she (Matilda) had lately heard from her (Matilda) sister.

`Yes,' replied she (Matilda). `She (Matilda) was at Paris when she (Matilda) wrote, and very well, and very happy.'

She (Matilda) spoke the last word emphatically, and with a glance impertinently sly. He (Weston) did not seem to notice it, but replied, with equal emphasis, and very seriously--

`I hope she (Matilda) will continue to be so.'

`Do you think it likely?' I ventured to inquire: for Matilda had started off in pursuit of her (Matilda) dog, that was chasing a leveret.

`I cannot tell,' replied he (Weston). `Sir Thomas may be a better man than I suppose; but, from all I have heard and seen, it seems a pity that one so young and gay, and--and interesting, to express many things by one word--whose greatest, if not her (Matilda) only fault, appears to be thoughtlessness--no trifling fault to be sure, since it renders the possessor liable to almost every other, and exposes him (Thomas) to so many temptations--but it seems a pity that she (Matilda) should be thrown away on such a man. It was her (Matilda) mother's wish, I suppose?'

`Yes; and her (Matilda) own too, I think, for she (Matilda) always laughed at my attempts to dissuade her (Matilda) from the step.'

`You did attempt it? Then, at least, you will have the satisfaction of knowing that it is no fault of yours, if any harm should come of it. As for Mrs. Murray, I don't know how she (Murray) can justify her (Murray) conduct: if I had sufficient acquaintance with her (Murray), I'd ask her (Murray).'

`It seems unnatural: but some people think rank and wealth the chief good; and, if they can secure that for their children, they think they have done their duty.'

`True: but is it not strange that persons of experience, who have been married themselves, should judge so falsely?' Matilda now came panting back, with the lacerated body of the young hare in her (Matilda) hand.

`Was it your intention to kill that hare, or to save it, Miss Murray?' asked Mr. Weston, apparently puzzled at her (Murray) gleeful countenance.

`I pretended to want to save it,' she (Murray) answered, honestly enough, `as it was so glaringly out of season; but I was better pleased to see it lolled. However, you can both witness that I couldn't help it: Prince was determined to have her (Murray); and he (Prince) clutched her (Murray) by the back, and killed her (Murray) in a minute! Wasn't it a noble chase?'

`Very! for a young lady after a leveret.'

There was a quiet sarcasm in the tone of his (Prince) reply which was not lost upon her (lady); she (lady) shrugged her (lady) shoulders, and, turning away with a significant `Humph!' asked me how I had enjoyed the fun. I replied that I saw no fun in the matter; but admitted that I had not observed the transaction very narrowly.

`Didn't you see how it doubled--just like an old hare? and didn't you hear it scream?'

`I'm happy to say I did not.'

`It cried out just like a child.'

`Poor little thing! What will you do with it?'

`Come along--I shall leave it in the first house we come to. I don't want to take it home, for fear papa should scold me for letting the dog kill it.'

Mr. Weston was now gone, and we too went on our way; but as we returned, after having deposited the hare in a farm-house, and demolished some spice-cake and currant-wine in exchange, we met him (Weston) returning also from the execution of his (Weston) mission, whatever it might be. He (Weston) carried in his (Weston) hand a cluster of beautiful bluebells, which he (Weston) offered to me; observing, with a smile, that though he (Weston) had seen so little of me for the last two months, he (Weston) had not forgotten that bluebells were numbered among my favourite flowers. It was done as a simple act of goodwill, without compliment or remarkable courtesy, or any look that could be construed into `reverential, tender adoration' -LRB-_vide_ Rosalie Murray-RRB-; but still, it was something to find my unimportant saying so well remembered: it was something that he (Weston) had noticed so accurately the time I had ceased to be visible.

`I was told,' said he (Weston), `that you were a perfect bookworm, Miss Grey: so completely absorbed in your studies that you were lost to every other pleasure.'

`Yes, and it's quite true!' cried Matilda.

`No, Mr. Weston: don't believe it: it's a scandalous libel. These young ladies are too fond of making random assertions at the expense of their friends; and you ought to be careful how you listen to them.'

`I hope _this_ assertion is groundless, at any rate.'

`Why? Do you particularly object to ladies studying?'

`No; but I object to anyone so devoting himself or herself to study, as to lose sight of everything else. Except under peculiar circumstances, I consider very close and constant study as a waste of time, and an injury to the mind as well as the body.'

`Well, I have neither the time nor the inclination for such transgressions.'

We parted again.

Well! what is there remarkable in all this? Why have I recorded it? Because, reader, it was important enough to give me a cheerful evening, a night of pleasing dreams, and a morning of felicitous hopes. Shallow-brained cheerfulness, foolish dreams, unfounded hopes, you would say; and I will not venture to deny it: suspicions to that effect arose too frequently in my own mind. But our wishes are like tinder: the flint and steel of circumstances are continually striking out sparks, which vanish immediately, unless they chance to fall upon the tinder of our wishes; then, they instantly ignite, and the flame of hope is kindled in a moment.

But alas! that very morning, my flickering flame of hope was dismally quenched by a letter from my mother, which spoke so seriously of my father's increasing illness, that I feared there was little or no chance of his (father) recovery; and, close at hand as the holidays were, I almost trembled lest they should come too late for me to meet him (father) in this world. Two days after, a letter from Mary told me his (father) life was despaired of, and his (father) end seemed fast approaching. Then, immediately, I sought permission to anticipate the vacation, and go without delay. Mrs. Murray stared, and wondered at the unwonted energy and boldness with which I urged the request, and thought there was no occasion to hurry; but finally gave me leave: stating, however, that there was `no need to be in such agitation about the matter--it might prove a false alarm after all; and if not--why, it was only in the common course of nature: we must all die some time; and I was not to suppose myself the only afflicted person in the world;' and concluding with saying I might have the phaeton to take me to O--. `And instead of _repining_, Miss Grey, be thankful for the _privileges_ you enjoy. There's many a poor clergyman whose family would be plunged into ruin by the event of his (clergyman) death; but you, you see, have influential friends ready to continue their patronage, and to show you every consideration.'

I thanked her (Grey) for her (Grey) `consideration,' and flew to my room to make some hurried preparations for my departure. My bonnet and shawl being on, and a few things hastily crammed into my largest trunk, I descended. But I might have done the work more leisurely, for no one else was in a hurry; and I had still a considerable time to wait for the phaeton. At length it came to the door, and I was off: but, oh, what a dreary journey was that! how utterly different from my former passages homewards! Being too late for the last coach to --, I had to hire a cab for ten miles, and then a car to take me over the rugged hills.

It was half-past ten before I reached home. They were not in bed.

My mother and sister both met me in the passage--sad--silent--pale! I was so much shocked and terror-stricken that I could not speak, to ask the information I so much longed yet dreaded to obtain.

`Agnes!' said my mother, struggling to repress some strong emotion.

`Oh, Agnes!' cried Mary, and burst into tears.

`How is he (Agnes)?' I asked, gasping for the answer.

`Dead!'

It was the reply I had anticipated: but the shock seemed none the less tremendous.

CHAPTER XIX--THE LETTER

My father's mortal remains had been consigned to the tomb; and we, with sad faces and sombre garments, sat lingering over the frugal breakfast-table, revolving plans for our future life. My mother's strong mind had not given way beneath even this affliction: her (mother) spirit, though crushed, was not broken. Mary's wish was that I should go back to Horton Lodge, and that our mother should come and live with her (mother) and Mr. Richardson at the vicarage: she (mother) affirmed that he (Richardson) wished it no less than herself, and that such an arrangement could not fail to benefit all parties; for my mother's society and experience would be of inestimable value to them, and they would do all they could to make her (mother) happy. But no arguments or entreaties could prevail: my mother was determined not to go. Not that she (mother) questioned, for a moment, the kind wishes and intentions of her (mother) daughter; but she (mother) affirmed that so long as God spared her (mother) health and strength, she (mother) would make use of them to earn her (mother) own livelihood, and be chargeable to no one; whether her (mother) dependence would be felt as a burden or not. If she (mother) could afford to reside as a lodger in--vicarage, she (mother) would choose that house before all others as the place of her (mother) abode; but not being so circumstanced, she (mother) would never come under its roof, except as an occasional visitor: unless sickness or calamity should render her (mother) assistance really needful, or until age or infirmity made her (mother) incapable of maintaining herself.

`No, Mary,' said she (Mary), `if Richardson and you have anything to spare, you must lay it aside for your family; and Agnes and I must gather honey for ourselves. Thanks to my having had daughters to educate, I have not forgotten my accomplishments. God willing, I will check this vain repining,' she (Agnes) said, while the tears coursed one another down her (Agnes) cheeks in spite of her (Agnes) efforts; but she (Agnes) wiped them away, and resolutely shaking back her (Agnes) head, continued, `I will exert myself, and look out for a small house, commodiously situated in some populous but healthy district, where we will take a few young ladies to board and educate--if we can get them--and as many day pupils as will come, or as we can manage to instruct. Your father's relations and old friends will be able to send us some pupils, or to assist us with their recommendations, no doubt: I shall not apply to my own. What say you to it, Agnes? will you be willing to leave your present situation and try?'

`Quite willing, mamma; and the money I have saved will do to furnish the house. It shall be taken from the bank directly.'

`When it is wanted: we must get the house, and settle on preliminaries first.'

Mary offered to lend the little she (Mary) possessed; but my mother declined it, saying that we must begin on an economical plan; and she (mother) hoped that the whole or part of mine, added to what we could get by the sale of the furniture, and what little our dear papa had contrived to lay aside for her (mother) since the debts were paid, would be sufficient to last us till Christmas; when, it was hoped, something would accrue from our united labours. It was finally settled that this should be our plan; and that inquiries and preparations should immediately be set on foot; and while my mother busied herself with these, I should return to Horton Lodge at the close of my four weeks' vacation, and give notice for my final departure when things were in train for the speedy commencement of our school.

We were discussing these affairs on the morning I have mentioned, about a fortnight after my father's death, when a letter was brought in for my mother, on beholding which the colour mounted to her (mother) face--lately pale enough with anxious watchings and excessive sorrow. `From my father!' murmured she (mother), as she (mother) hastily tore off the cover. It was many years since she (mother) had heard from any of her (mother) own relations before. Naturally wondering what the letter might contain, I watched her (mother) countenance while she (mother) read it, and was somewhat surprised to see her (mother) bite her (mother) lip and knit her (mother) brows as if in anger. When she (mother) had done, she (mother) somewhat irreverently cast it on the table, saying with a scornful smile,--`Your grandpapa has been so kind as to write to me. He (lip) says he (lip) has no doubt I have long repented of my

``unfortunate marriage,'' (I)
and if I will only acknowledge this, and confess I was wrong in neglecting his (lip) advice, and that I have justly suffered for it, he (lip) will make a lady of me once again--if that be possible after my long degradation--and remember my girls in his (lip) will.
